  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e20fb47b-afdb-4a27-b3ff-e5f2f43cb563'

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6610436192207077611"><i>Doña Bárbara</i>, en la que la llanura venezolana es el personaje central, es la obra de mayor éxito de Rómulo Gallegos. En ella dramatiza el conflicto entre civilización y barbarie que a su parecer definía el ser de Venezuela y su realidad. El final feliz simboliza un futuro esperanzador para el país.</span>

<div id="review_666117355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/666117355" />
    <a title="Alejandro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328672389p2/665584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/666117355?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alejandro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alejandro" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro">Alejandro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789684528949" href="/book/show/9724566-do-a-b-rbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer666117355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15152977731261756349"><i>This review is bilingual. First you can find the English version and at the end of that one, you will find the Spanish version.</i><br/><br/><br/><b>
  <u>This is my favorite Latin American book!!!</u>
</b><br/><br/>Maybe since I am from Costa Rica, I should pick as my favorite Latin American book, something from my own country. This book is by an author from Venezuela. And indeed I like a lot of books written by authors from my country. However, to choose a single book for my "Favorites" virtual shelf, when it's down to pick just one bo</span>
  <span id="freeText15152977731261756349" style="display:none"><i>This review is bilingual. First you can find the English version and at the end of that one, you will find the Spanish version.</i><br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>This is my favorite Latin American book!!!</u>
</b><br /><br />Maybe since I am from Costa Rica, I should pick as my favorite Latin American book, something from my own country. This book is by an author from Venezuela. And indeed I like a lot of books written by authors from my country. However, to choose a single book for my "Favorites" virtual shelf, when it's down to pick just one book in Spanish, I have to choose this book <i>Doña Barbara</i>.<br /><br />Also, it's the only book that I had to read in high school as homework that appears in the "Favorites" virtual shelf that as I commented in other review of books in that shelf, I decided to limit that shelf to only 10 books. To denote how important are really these books to me.<br /><br />From all the books that I had to read in school and high school, definitely this one is my favorite of those. <br /><br />This novel tells the story of a woman, a "man-eater" woman, whose name is Barbara and everybody calls her "Doña Barbara", "doña" means like "mrs". But that title here denotes also the respect and fear that everybody, specially the men, have for her. And her first name "Barbara", is a real name used by many women but in Spanish also means "wild" and this is crutial in the story, since she lives in the deep of the jungle around the Arauca River. She is a landlady, powerful, rich and ruthless. When she was young, she was raped by pirates, also losing her first love, so she became bitter and hating to all men. <br /><br />The other main character in this story is "Santos Luzardo" whose name is another word game, like with Doña Barbara, only a little more tricky, "Santos" means "saint" and "Luzardo" means like "light", and so he is like the saint light of civilization and progress that it's entering to the wilderness of the jungle. <br /><br />The shock when those powerful characters meet is the very heart of the story. And don't worry if you think that I spoiled you the book, all that it's only the introduction of the main characters. The story is just beginning. Since this a really popular novel from Latin America, I am sure that it's available in English too, but I felt important to explain you the names of the main characters since its symbolic importance can be lost in translation.<br /><br /><br />--0--<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>¡¡¡Este es mi libro Latinoamericano favorito!!!</u>
</b><br /><br />Puede ser dado que soy de Costa Rica, que yo debería haber elegido como mi libro Latinoamericano favorito, alguno de mi propio país. Este libro es por un autor de Venezuela. Y en efecto, me gustan muchos libros escritos por autores de mi país. Sin embargo, para elegir un solo libro para mi estante virtual de "Favoritos", cuando se trata de elegir solo uno en Español. Yo tengo que elegir este libro <i>Doña Barbara</i>. <br /><br />También, es el único libro que tuve que leer en el colegio como tarea que aparece en mi estante virtual de "Favoritos" que como ya comenté en otras críticas de libros de ese estante, yo decidí limitar ese estante a solo 10 libros. Para denotar cuan importantes son realmente esos libros para mí.<br /><br />De todos los libros que tuve que leer en la escuela y el colegio, definitivamente este es mi favorito.<br /><br />Esta novela cuenta la historia de una mujer, una mujer "come-hombres", cuyo nombre es Bárbara y todos le llaman "Doña Bárbara", "doña" es como señora. Pero este título aquí denota también el respeto y miedo que todos, especialmente los hombres, tienen por ella. Y su primer nombre "Bárbara", es un verdadero nombre usado por muchas mujeres pero en Español también significa "salvaje" y esto es crucial en la historia, dado que ella vive en lo profundo de la jungla alrededor del Río del Arauca. Ella es una terrateniente, poderosa, rica y despiadada. Cuando ella era joven, fué violada por piratas, también perdiendo a su primer amor, así que ella se amargó odiando a todos los hombres.<br /><br />El otro personaje principal en esta historia es "Santos Luzardo" cuyo nombre es otro juego de palabras, como con Doña Bárbara, solo que un poco más triquiñuelo, "Santos" significa "santo" y "Luzardo" significa algo como "luz", y así el es como la santa luz de la civilización y el progreso que entra en la barbarie de la jungla.<br /><br />El impacto cuando estos poderosos personajes se encuentran es el corazón mismo de la historia. Y no se preocupen si creen que les arruiné el libro. Todo esto es solo la introducción de los personajes principales. La historia está apenas comenzando. Dado que esta es una novel realmente popular en Latin America, estoy seguro que deben haber versiones en Inglés también, pero siento importante explicarles los nombres de los personajes principales cuya importancia simbólica puede perderse en la traducción.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15152977731261756349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1036074838" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1036074838" />
    <a title="Harry Rutherford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1385097-harry-rutherford"><img alt="Harry Rutherford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217536148p2/1385097.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1036074838?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harry Rutherford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harry Rutherford" href="/user/show/1385097-harry-rutherford">Harry Rutherford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18905257-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1385097-harry-rutherford?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1385097-harry-rutherford?shelf=south-america">south-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1036074838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17571388462366925685">An interesting fact about Rómulo Gallegos: he was the first democratically elected president of Venezuela, in 1948 (although only for a few months before losing power to a coup d’état). He was a writer before he was a politician; <i>Doña Bárbara</i> was published in 1929. It is, of course, my book from Venezuela for the <a href='http://heracliteanfire.net/2008/08/01/reading-is-a-way-around-the-world/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Read The World challenge.</a><br/><br/>I didn’t choose it because the author was president of Venezuela. I was more attracted by the fact that it has been made into a movie twice and a telenovela thr</span>
  <span id="freeText17571388462366925685" style="display:none">An interesting fact about Rómulo Gallegos: he was the first democratically elected president of Venezuela, in 1948 (although only for a few months before losing power to a coup d’état). He was a writer before he was a politician; <i>Doña Bárbara</i> was published in 1929. It is, of course, my book from Venezuela for the <a href="http://heracliteanfire.net/2008/08/01/reading-is-a-way-around-the-world/" rel="nofollow noopener">Read The World challenge.</a><br /><br />I didn’t choose it because the author was president of Venezuela. I was more attracted by the fact that it has been made into a movie twice and a telenovela three times. And that suggests a novel with a good story to tell.<br /><br />It is indeed a rollicking yarn, full of love, lust, jealousy, dancing, cattle rustling, chicanery, revenge, murder, sweeping landscapes, colourful birds, and manly men riding across the plains. The portrayal of women is slightly more problematic, in that there are only two major female characters, and one is pure, virginal, innocent, passive and ineffectual, while the other — the eponymous Doña Bàrbara — is manipulative, ruthless, corrupt, witchy, and uses sex as a weapon.<br /><br />To be fair, Doña Bàrbara is a terrific character, a sort of cowboy Lady Macbeth. Or Macbeth, Lady Macbeth and the witches rolled into one. And in best superhero fashion, she is given a backstory of childhood trauma to account for her villainy. It’s just the contrast with the young Marisela which implies a rather narrow role for what a good woman can be like.<br /><br />Despite Gallegos’s later career, this didn’t strike me as a particularly political novel in the same way as, for example, a lot of the post-colonial fiction I’ve been reading. Although you can see how it could be a part of a developing Venezuelan nationalism, because it is very much a novel about a place and a culture; the plains and the plainsmen who raise cattle there. I could see it forming part of a Venezuelan identity, rather as other cowboys did in the US.<br /><br />However, the Wikipedia entry for <i>Doña Bàrbara</i> notes that ‘it was because of the book’s criticisms of the regime of longtime dictator Juan Vicente Gómez that [Gallegos] was forced to flee the country’. So I obviously missed some nuances. I guess the portrayal of political corruption — even though mainly at the local level in the book — is the kind of thing that dictators get annoyed by. They’re a notoriously thin-skinned bunch.<br /><br />Anyway, I enjoyed it. And despite the tone of my comments, not just as a slightly melodramatic yarn — although that was enjoyable — but as a literary novel. It has an evocative sense of place, atmospheric set pieces, strong characters. Good stuff.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17571388462366925685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1400489480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1400489480" />
    <a title="Marce Matamoros" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40514718-marce-matamoros"><img alt="Marce Matamoros" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424542951p2/40514718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1400489480?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marce Matamoros" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marce Matamoros" href="/user/show/40514718-marce-matamoros">Marce Matamoros</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1400489480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4515735346065832775">Though I never read it in high school, Dona Barbara is a staple lecture across Latin American schools all over, and maybe this fact kept me away from it for a long time, as normally the readings assigned as part of a scholar program tend to be boring. I couldn;t be more wrong with it. <br/><br/>Short, but with an amazing narrative pace, the book tells us the story of Don Barbara, a cruel and vindictive woman who is know as the "cacica" (chief) of the Arauca, in the venezuelan "llanos", due to her ferocity</span>
  <span id="freeText4515735346065832775" style="display:none">Though I never read it in high school, Dona Barbara is a staple lecture across Latin American schools all over, and maybe this fact kept me away from it for a long time, as normally the readings assigned as part of a scholar program tend to be boring. I couldn;t be more wrong with it. <br /><br />Short, but with an amazing narrative pace, the book tells us the story of Don Barbara, a cruel and vindictive woman who is know as the "cacica" (chief) of the Arauca, in the venezuelan "llanos", due to her ferocity, unscrupulous nature, and her apparent supernatural powers. But behind that facade, lies a woman with tremendous grief due to a horrible experience in her youth, which molded her personality and brings her the painful memory of a one time love, of the person she might have become if reality hadn;t been so crude to her. As a character, her nature is contrasting, as she might be interpreted as a feminist heroine, being brave enough to make other respect herself in a sexist environment, while at the same time being the damisel who wants to be rescued from her somber fate by a righteous man, represented in the figure of Santos Luzardo.<br /><br />It never gets boring, I can guarantee, and though it may remind you of a latin american soap opera at times, it never falls into that melodramatic space. As well, descriptions of the life in the "llanos" are vivid and full of sensations. I recommend it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4515735346065832775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2571632374" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2571632374" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/501971-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592065078p2/501971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2571632374?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/501971-jeff">Jeff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">South American novel seekers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jeff by: </span>
      PBS's Great American Read
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/501971-jeff?shelf=great-american-read">great-american-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2571632374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1114983930811756036">I suspect many (most?) folks who read this book or added it to their To Read shelf in late 2018 did so because of PBS's show <i>The Great American Read</i>. <br/><br/>I had to read the English translation by Chicago University Press, from 1931. As such, much of the colloquial speech was lost on me. Then again, some sections were nicely lyrical, which i assume came thru nicely from the original Spanish. Overall, a somewhat unnerving experience.<br/><br/>The novel could also be titled <i>Santos Luzardo</i>. He's really the main ch</span>
  <span id="freeText1114983930811756036" style="display:none">I suspect many (most?) folks who read this book or added it to their To Read shelf in late 2018 did so because of PBS's show <i>The Great American Read</i>. <br /><br />I had to read the English translation by Chicago University Press, from 1931. As such, much of the colloquial speech was lost on me. Then again, some sections were nicely lyrical, which i assume came thru nicely from the original Spanish. Overall, a somewhat unnerving experience.<br /><br />The novel could also be titled <i>Santos Luzardo</i>. He's really the main character. I'm not surprised that a male author focused more on his male protagonist even while naming his novel for the female antagonist. Or maybe i've just got my male blinkers on. A review that i really liked suggested that a novel truly from Doña Barbara's perspective would be much more interesting these days. If <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25022.Jean_Rhys" title="Jean Rhys" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean Rhys</a> had been Venezuelan, she might've written something called <i>Don Luzardo</i>. Or <i>Marisela del Llano</i>? or <i>Llaneras</i>?<br /><br />The Doña definitely deserved better treatment. As male-centric as this book was, i don't believe it implies women=barbaric and men=civilized. <br /><br />I can stand behind the allegory of education and the rule of law triumphing over foolish adherence to counterproductive traditions. <br /><br />Bottom line, though, i was only slightly invested in the main characters' failures and successes. Probably the result of Gallegos's totally different world than mine. And a little bit being lost in translation.<br /><br />By the numbers:<br /><a href="https://www.pbs.org/the-great-american-read/results/" rel="nofollow noopener">Great American Read</a>: #100 of 100. (cf <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2657.To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird by Harper Lee" rel="nofollow noopener">To Kill a Mockingbird</a> #1; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/587007.Mind_Invaders" title="Mind Invaders by Dave Hunt" rel="nofollow noopener">Mind Invaders</a> #95!?)<br />Goodreads ratings (as of January 26, 2019): 3,082 (TKaM=3.8million; Mind Invaders=139)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1114983930811756036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2400810611" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2400810611" />
    <a title="Pam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8580024-pam"><img alt="Pam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603385327p2/8580024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2400810611?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pam" href="/user/show/8580024-pam">Pam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=translated-lit">translated-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=latin-american-literature">latin-american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=2018-aty-challenge">2018-aty-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8580024-pam?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2400810611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14969916685584125624">I decided to read this book because it was one of the few on the PBS Great American Read list that I had never heard of. I am so glad that I did! I thoroughly enjoyed it and felt that I had been transported to the llano (prairie) in Venezuela, roping cattle, riding horses, and hunting alligators! I don't always appreciate a lot of description of the setting, but in this case, it definitely enhanced the reading experience. The language was beautiful!<br/><br/>I was a little surprised that so much of the bo</span>
  <span id="freeText14969916685584125624" style="display:none">I decided to read this book because it was one of the few on the PBS Great American Read list that I had never heard of. I am so glad that I did! I thoroughly enjoyed it and felt that I had been transported to the llano (prairie) in Venezuela, roping cattle, riding horses, and hunting alligators! I don't always appreciate a lot of description of the setting, but in this case, it definitely enhanced the reading experience. The language was beautiful!<br /><br />I was a little surprised that so much of the book was about Santos Luzardo's experiences, rather than his cousin Doña Bárbara. (Ultimately, it was about her, though!) Luzardo returns to the llano to reclaim his family's land and cattle. In doing so, he has to deal with the cruel and treacherous (but beautiful) Doña Bárbara, the cacique (chief) of the Aracua. Barbara's horrific experience as a young woman shaped her personality and her attitude towards men in a way that turned her into a vengeful and dangerous woman (with supposed magic powers). She meets her match in Luzardo, her enemy yet a man that she respects. The events that transpire between the two of them and their peons (workers) make for a great story. Read the book and discover for yourself. It may not be the story you expect! It certainly wasn't for me. <br /><br />Here are a few quotes that I liked:<br /><br />"They ran in two contrary currents of reflection and impulse, determination and hesitation."<br />"Progress will come to the Plain and barbarism will be conquered and retreat."<br />"Those women succeed with everything they start out to do, because men are all fools." <br />And, a recurring idea: "All things return whence they came."<br /><br />Another aspect of the book that I liked was learning a few new Spanish words. Some examples include: balata, guaracha, peon, carpincho, cacica or cacique, and blancaje.<br /><br />I truly hope that more non-Spanish speaking readers discover this Spanish classic. It is a real treasure! It's a 5 star rating for me. The only thing that would have been better for me is if I could have read it in Spanish! Tal vez algun dia. (Maybe some day.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14969916685584125624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18293283741275361964">2.5 raised to a 3. Plot is hard to summarize, but broadly, this is set in the 1930s and concerns a man, Santos Luzardo, trying to recover his ranch land on the plains of Venezuela most of which has been swallowed up by a witch-like woman, Doña Barbara, and her evil henchmen while Santos has been away and dependent on an unscrupulous overseer. We are given a good deal of the local color. This is a seminal work in Latin American literature, with the beginnings of "magic realism", developed more fu</span>
  <span id="freeText18293283741275361964" style="display:none">2.5 raised to a 3. Plot is hard to summarize, but broadly, this is set in the 1930s and concerns a man, Santos Luzardo, trying to recover his ranch land on the plains of Venezuela most of which has been swallowed up by a witch-like woman, Doña Barbara, and her evil henchmen while Santos has been away and dependent on an unscrupulous overseer. We are given a good deal of the local color. This is a seminal work in Latin American literature, with the beginnings of "magic realism", developed more fully in later works. As far as characters: names are symbolic: "Santos"--holy or saintly; "Barbara", a wild, untamed force. There's even an Ugly American, Señor Danger, one of the villains. Besides the regaining of Santos's land, a big theme is Transformation to the positive of the personalities of the main female characters.<br /><br />This may read better in the original Spanish; I felt this English translation was clunky.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3511521932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12332639435441452468">3.5*** (rounded up)<br/><br/>This classic of South American literature was first published in 1929 and virtually forgotten by US readers. It first came to my attention through Public Broadcasting System’s Great American Read program in 2019. <br/><br/>It is an epic tale of two cousins who are fighting for control of a vast estate / ranch in Venezuela. Doña Barbara is beautiful and powerful. At her core she hates men for the way she was used and abused as a young girl. She has earned a reputation as a witch and is w</span>
  <span id="freeText12332639435441452468" style="display:none">3.5*** (rounded up)<br /><br />This classic of South American literature was first published in 1929 and virtually forgotten by US readers. It first came to my attention through Public Broadcasting System’s Great American Read program in 2019. <br /><br />It is an epic tale of two cousins who are fighting for control of a vast estate / ranch in Venezuela. Doña Barbara is beautiful and powerful. At her core she hates men for the way she was used and abused as a young girl. She has earned a reputation as a witch and is widely known for using, abusing and tossing aside her lovers as whim and her needs dictate. She fits perfectly into the wild, untamed landscape of the <i>llano</i> (prairie) and the surrounding swamps, bogs and jungles. <br /><br />In contrast, we have Santos Luzardo, who, while born on the plains has long left for an education in the city. Now he returns to reclaim his inheritance – land and cattle. But he retains the manners and customs of his city education. Their battle is both cerebral (who can outwit the other?), and physical, involving seduction, chicanery, violence, gamesmanship, cunning and some magic as well. <br /><br />The novel is an early example of magical realism and Gallegos weaves together adventure, fantasy, and romance. He uses vivid description and outlandish storytelling (I loved the one-eyed monster alligator!) to bring this era to life. The various outdoor scenes were particularly exciting – I could practically taste the grit, feel the dust in my nostrils, hear the roar of stampeding herd, and smell the smoke of an uncontrolled wildfire.<br /><br />If there was an element that made me downgrade my rating it was the story of Marisela, and the way that Doña Barbara came to terms with that character. It was rather melodramatic and very soap-opera-ish. <br /><br />In the introduction, Larry McMurtry writes: “[Doña Barbara] is, in her way, a tragic heroine, seeking to attract a decent lover, while giving herself day and night to very coarse lovers indeed. She is, however very vividly drawn, a Bovary of the <i>llano</i>.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12332639435441452468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2143849112" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2143849112" />
    <a title="Liz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3252127-liz"><img alt="Liz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369262994p2/3252127.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz" href="/user/show/3252127-liz">Liz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3252127-liz?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3252127-liz?shelf=latin-american-fiction">latin-american-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2143849112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13439639681965526461">This was good; I would probably recommend to those interested in LatAm fiction with some caveats.<br/><br/>The good:<br/>- all the descriptions of the llanos were great. I fucking love the llanos! In that respect I am def this book's target audience.<br/>- the melodrama was very entertaining<br/>- lots of dialogue<br/>- all the description of daily life and seasonal activities reminded me of "Little House on the Prarie."<br/>- I also really liked all the parts about llano customs and superstitions. I fucking love the llanos!! Did</span>
  <span id="freeText13439639681965526461" style="display:none">This was good; I would probably recommend to those interested in LatAm fiction with some caveats.<br /><br />The good:<br />- all the descriptions of the llanos were great. I fucking love the llanos! In that respect I am def this book's target audience.<br />- the melodrama was very entertaining<br />- lots of dialogue<br />- all the description of daily life and seasonal activities reminded me of "Little House on the Prarie."<br />- I also really liked all the parts about llano customs and superstitions. I fucking love the llanos!! Did I miss my calling to be a cowgirl? <br />- I forgot that Hugo Chavez is from the llanos!! This book was apparently one of his favorites and the origin of his nickname ("Mr. Danger") for George W. Bush. Very interesting!<br />- Also interesting to me that the writer later became president of Venezuela. I mean, can you imagine a modern-day politician writing a novel even as half as good as this one? Obama could do it though. <br />- The ending was a bit ambiguous, which was great!<br /><br />The meh<br /> - The melodrama. This is a pretty corny book so you're either feeling it or you're not.<br />- The symbolism is a bit on the nose. Subtle, this book is not. It'd make a fun Netflix series though! I'm not surprised there's various film/telenovela versions.<br />- You learn a LOT about cattle in this book, and there were definitely some moments when I was like, damn, this is a LOT of info about cattle... <br />- I had trouble telling the secondary characters apart. <br />- I can't imagine reading this in Spanish. Sooooo much llano vocabulary. I would not be able to deal. <br /><br />In conclusion, I wouldn't tell everyone to read this book, but I will definitely tell some of my gal pals, "have you heard of this book, it's worth checking out, it's pretty interesting and I found it enjoyable." Didn't LOVE it, but I am glad I read it.</span>

  <span id="freeText18207770508077464915" style="display:none">Dona Barbara is a woman who has come to hate men for what they have done to her. Rather than becoming a victim and living in shame she decides to turn the tables and make them her victims instead. She learns the arts of plants from the native Indians in her country and using superstitions she takes control and becomes a prosperous rancher. Though much of her ranch is gained through corruption of public officials and dirty dealing. It is when Santos comes back to take Altimeira back that she finally meets a man she can respect is who reminds her of her love for the young man who was murdered before her cruel rape . It is when at the end that you see she is someone who merits compassion as she stays her hand and pulls the gun down. It is the image of her daughter happy as she once was.<br />The writing is very good.<br /> "The Plain is at once lovely and fearful. It holds,side by side, beautiful life and hideous death. The latter lurks everywhere, but no one fears it. The Plain frightens , but the fear which the Plain inspires is not terror which chills the heart; it is hot, like the fever lying in the marshes".<br />I also found the room which had been locked up for some 13 years when upon opening it..." A rush of stale,foul air drove Santos back. Something black and loathsome -a bag coming out of the darkness- fluttered against the candle and extinguished it. "<br />Plus some of the vocabulary was interesting with words like pettifogging, autonomasia, sententiously, and entelechy to name a few. Along with Spanish words like Gua!</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shelly L" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shelly L" href="/user/show/38761322-shelly-l">Shelly L</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/32462420-do-a-b-rbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38761322-shelly-l?shelf=gunsmoke">gunsmoke</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38761322-shelly-l?shelf=100_pbs_gr_amer_reads">100_pbs_gr_amer_reads</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2953415011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2722759582083325617">Don't fence me in. This is about taming the flaming will of a woman, and whether ranching is best done with the brutality and cunning of the open range, or with laws and fences and civilized ideas. I learned brutal and interesting things from many a lavish description. For example, if you say you are bushed, you are making a gelding reference! You might mean you’re tired, but you’ll also be implying a certain gendered powerlessness. A gelded bull not only lays in the bush all day, he’s made lazy</span>
  <span id="freeText2722759582083325617" style="display:none">Don't fence me in. This is about taming the flaming will of a woman, and whether ranching is best done with the brutality and cunning of the open range, or with laws and fences and civilized ideas. I learned brutal and interesting things from many a lavish description. For example, if you say you are bushed, you are making a gelding reference! You might mean you’re tired, but you’ll also be implying a certain gendered powerlessness. A gelded bull not only lays in the bush all day, he’s made lazy and impotent and female by having his balls chopped off. Trimmed to a "bush," you see. Ah, the Llano is a manly man’s world — but for legendary femme fatale, Doña Barbara. Now, a plainsman born and raised — but also urban-educated — returns to his dark ancestral ranch to bring long mismanagement into the light. Though at first we’re led to think of him as a city slicker who won't last a minute against cutthroats like the Wizard and Witchypoo, he’s a good shot, has the requisite cajones, and is easy on the eyes to boot. He ends up playing Prince Charming in this vaquero-infused retelling of <i>Snow White.</i> A furious Doña Barbara plays the magnificent, magical Queen, whilst her own daughter transforms into a fresh and beautiful rival. Enjoyed this book for all the reasons. Yet, for a tale named after our evil queen, we spend precious little time with her. And in the end, she melts away meekly, as if by the water of her own tears.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2722759582083325617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10889981518961217495">Doña barbara is a historical novel that describes the Venezuelan society prior to its modernization.<br/><br/>Gallegos uses very symbolic characters and actions in his description of a chaotic country ruled by few. Santos Luzardo returns to the plains of Venezuela to recover land that has been taken by the main character: Doña Barbara, a woman full of resentment representing the barbarie.<br/>Luzardo proposes a division of the properties with fencing.   <span id="freeText10889981518961217495" style="display:none">Doña barbara is a historical novel that describes the Venezuelan society prior to its modernization.<br /><br />Gallegos uses very symbolic characters and actions in his description of a chaotic country ruled by few. Santos Luzardo returns to the plains of Venezuela to recover land that has been taken by the main character: Doña Barbara, a woman full of resentment representing the barbarie.<br />Luzardo proposes a division of the properties with fencing. He is the civilization coming from the city to change things forever. <br />The action happens slowly, but the pearl is in the dialogues. I am not sure how much would be lost in translation if this novel is read in English, but in Spanish this is one of the best novels written in South America. A couple of movies were made after it.<br />This is a highly intellectual novel and certainly not one to provide immediate satisfaction. but if you have patience and would like to explore a very unique narrative I think you will enjoy it.</span>

<div id="review_2592661609" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2592661609" />
    <a title="Alicia Herrington" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15360068-alicia-herrington"><img alt="Alicia Herrington" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1355167506p2/15360068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2592661609?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alicia Herrington" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alicia Herrington" href="/user/show/15360068-alicia-herrington">Alicia Herrington</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15360068-alicia-herrington?shelf=2019-challenge-great-american-read">2019-challenge-great-american-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15360068-alicia-herrington?shelf=2019-reading-challenge">2019-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2592661609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15050585015684254008">This year for one of my personal reading challenges I'm trying something I'm calling's "The Great American-Read-Through". For it I'm trying to read (or reread as the case may be) through as much of The Great American Read list as I can. I'm doing this because I want to understand why the books on the list are so beloved, even the ones I personally dislike (50 shades, Flowers in the Attic, Twilight, Left Behind, etc.) I'm starting at #100 and working backwards to #1. By the end of this year I'd l</span>
  <span id="freeText15050585015684254008" style="display:none">This year for one of my personal reading challenges I'm trying something I'm calling's "The Great American-Read-Through". For it I'm trying to read (or reread as the case may be) through as much of The Great American Read list as I can. I'm doing this because I want to understand why the books on the list are so beloved, even the ones I personally dislike (50 shades, Flowers in the Attic, Twilight, Left Behind, etc.) I'm starting at #100 and working backwards to #1. By the end of this year I'd like to make it through at least #51. This may not happen though as there are a lot of books in the back half of the list that were either nominated as a series (i.e. Alex Cross) or are a part of a series (i.e. Ghost). In either case, I want to read the whole series, because I think that that will give me a better understanding of the book/series popularity than a single example will. <br /><br />Anyway, without further ado, here's the book that came in at #100, Doña Barbara.<br /><br />Librarian: I'm sure than many libraries already have a copy of this book. I know that it's unlikely that they have more than one copy (unless it's a university library and this book is being taught in a South American literature class), and many of those copies may only have been acquired when the book was nominated for the Great American Read, but I do hope that making the list changes that. It's such a gorgeous book that I'm sure that more people would fall in love with it if they had a chance. I hope that librarians across the country got a chance to really talk this one up during the Read this past summer.<br />Reader: What a beautiful book. I'll admit that I hadn't read this novel before. In truth, I hadn't even heard of it. I'm glad though, that it was on the list and I had the chance to read it.<br />In reading it I absolutely understand why it would deserve to be on a list of best loved novels. The prose is gorgeous and super poetical. As I read, I found myself constantly highlighting favorite passages. The words dance across the page. Both the author, and the translator do an impressive job of drawing the reader into the prose.<br />Why isn't this one on more high school reading lists? (Any high school reading lists? I mean I've never heard of students reading this one, but maybe they just don't read it around me.) <br /><br />Reading Challenge: Great American Read Through<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15050585015684254008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2298038084" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2298038084" />
    <a title="Lola Andrews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/77635181-lola-andrews"><img alt="Lola Andrews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542723719p2/77635181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2298038084?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lola Andrews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lola Andrews" href="/user/show/77635181-lola-andrews">Lola Andrews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=not-living-authors">not-living-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=1920-s">1920-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=books-turned-into-tv-shows">books-turned-into-tv-shows</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77635181-lola-andrews?shelf=venezuelan-authors">venezuelan-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2298038084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9375725112002617407">I spent some of my high school years living in Venezuela (4, to be exact), and this was, at the time, a mandatory reading (along with other Rómulo Gallegos titles, such as La Trepadora and Canaima). Rómulo Gallegos, who was also at one point the president of the country (in fact, the first president to be selected by direct, secret and universal voting in Venezuela) has one topic which all his novels directly or indirectly talk about, which is the fight between savagery and education. Education </span>
  <span id="freeText9375725112002617407" style="display:none">I spent some of my high school years living in Venezuela (4, to be exact), and this was, at the time, a mandatory reading (along with other Rómulo Gallegos titles, such as La Trepadora and Canaima). Rómulo Gallegos, who was also at one point the president of the country (in fact, the first president to be selected by direct, secret and universal voting in Venezuela) has one topic which all his novels directly or indirectly talk about, which is the fight between savagery and education. Education always won in his novels, since he was very much trying to make a point to a Venezuela that was developing from an occupied country into an independent Republic filled with untapped talent (and it did work, for a while... now, well, I better not touch certain subjects that have most of my high school friends running away from their own country to settle somewhere else).<br /><br />Any case, Doña Bárbara is probably his best novel, and one that has certainly entered the pantheon of great Latin American narrative, and which I highly recommend as a read for everyone who wants to wrap their head around the idiosyncrasies of the region.<br /><br />Aside from its thematic importance, Gallegos has a fabulous talent to paint the Venezuelan llanos as they are, making you feel as if you're walking the very same dusty paths the characters are walking. He evokes places and times beautifully, and populates his worlds of archetypal characters that feel, nonetheless, very much like people. Thus, you'll find savagery personified in the character of Doña Bárbara, hope and purity in her daughter Marisela and civilization and education in Santos Luzardo. Along the ride, you'll meet several typical characters such as Juan Primito, a loyal servant with all the heart Doña Bárbara has lost, or Mister Danger, representing the invasive foreign presence within the country (and who Hugo Chavez would call upon in his speeches to speak against the USA often).<br /><br />I will say, as a female, there is something that always keeps me a bit embittered with this novel. After all, savagery comes at the hand of Doña Bárbara's character, a woman that suffered through trauma very early on in her life (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she's raped by several men, and her first love is killed <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) and who survived to rule herself and her possessions, and who eventually falls under the spell of the great civilized man, Santos Luzardo. Alas, out of the fight comes Marisela, representing the hope of holding on to the purity of wildness while giving space to education. (It's just, Doña Bárbara is such a fantastic character, that coming into this from a more modern perspective, you kinda just wanna see her burn shit up, fuck the themes represented within the novel, but I suppose that would fail at the intellectual endeavor that is the whole point of the book).<br /><br />Whatever the case, Doña Bárbara is one of the best characters to ever grace the written pages, and I heartily recommend this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9375725112002617407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2448995150" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2448995150" />
    <a title="Claudia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70211-claudia"><img alt="Claudia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306772918p2/70211.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2448995150?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudia" href="/user/show/70211-claudia">Claudia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70211-claudia?shelf=classics-i-should-have-read-years-b">classics-i-should-have-read-years-b</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70211-claudia?shelf=2018-challenge-155-books">2018-challenge-155-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70211-claudia?shelf=summer-books">summer-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2448995150" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15009270670831380883">When I looked at the PBS list of favorite 100 books, I did not recognize this author or title...so, I've been spending my summer in South American magic realism...sorta. Had to abandon 100 YEARS for now, but had a great time with Zafon, and I discovered this gem.<br/><br/>This is an epic battle between two characters...cousins...a man and a woman...the past and the future...good and evil. Santos returns to the plains of Venezuela to reclaim his birthright and try out his modern ideas of ranching and livin</span>
  <span id="freeText15009270670831380883" style="display:none">When I looked at the PBS list of favorite 100 books, I did not recognize this author or title...so, I've been spending my summer in South American magic realism...sorta. Had to abandon 100 YEARS for now, but had a great time with Zafon, and I discovered this gem.<br /><br />This is an epic battle between two characters...cousins...a man and a woman...the past and the future...good and evil. Santos returns to the plains of Venezuela to reclaim his birthright and try out his modern ideas of ranching and living on the wild llanos. Dona Barbara, an older woman, a man-eater purported to be a witch, has been craftily absorbing more and more of Santos's land into her own adjoining ranch. As a witch, she is devoted to the old ways of manipulation and intimidation.<br /><br />Barbara's character is fascinating...abused as a child, she made the conscious decision to never love, never give herself, never open herself up to any loving relationship, including her daughter, whom Santos finds living in a hovel with her father....Marisela adds another view of the plains, showing how, without proper attention, everything can go to Hell in a flash. But with that attention, it can thrive and show all its potential. But the question remains: why go to those lengths?<br /><br />Minor characters are fantastic...mealy-mouthed government workers, rough-and-tumble cowboys with bawdy senses of humor, supercilious officials, witches, henchmen...and a one-eyed crocodile. They all give us the sense of what this place can produce.<br /><br />Probably, in my mind, the most important 'character' is the setting...not the plains of our experience -- high and dry and windy. This plain is lush, surrounded by fecund rivers, swamps, quicksand. Wildlife in abundance. Santos is determined to tame the land with his modern ideas, Barbara is content to live within it and adjust to the seasons. I could almost feel the land breathing in and out.<br /><br />This book and Gallegos are credited with beginning the magic realism interest in Latin American literature...and magic is important. We must accept magical events and characters without question. But this setting is so new to me, that was not hard at all.<br /><br />Gallegos, as an artist, became Venezuela's first democratically-elected President...but just for a few months. I need to know more about him and his career. Supposedly, there are pointed criticisms of the current regime in this book, but I was oblivious of them as I rushed through this very unorthodox love story...and it is a love story. Not MADAME BOVARY, as Larry McMurtry says...it's more. And Barbara would eat Emma for breakfast and not even belch. Just saying, Barbara is one fierce woman.<br /><br />What a wonderful find! </span>
  <a data-text-id="15009270670831380883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2481801061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2481801061" />
    <a title="Malcolm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/783700-malcolm"><img alt="Malcolm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442473238p2/783700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2481801061?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malcolm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malcolm" href="/user/show/783700-malcolm">Malcolm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2481801061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9050207666762092826">Perhaps the greatest testimony to Rómulo Gallegos is the very rich literary prize named after him in honor of his work and influence. The prize has been awarded to Mario Vargas Llosa, Gabriel García Márquez, Roberto Bolaño and other masterful authors whose powerful books are said to be the cornerstones of magical realism and South American literature.<br/><br/>Sadly, we seldom hear Gallegos' 1929 novel "Doña Barbara" mentioned in the same company as the well-known books that have received the Gallegos pri</span>
  <span id="freeText9050207666762092826" style="display:none">Perhaps the greatest testimony to Rómulo Gallegos is the very rich literary prize named after him in honor of his work and influence. The prize has been awarded to Mario Vargas Llosa, Gabriel García Márquez, Roberto Bolaño and other masterful authors whose powerful books are said to be the cornerstones of magical realism and South American literature.<br /><br />Sadly, we seldom hear Gallegos' 1929 novel "Doña Barbara" mentioned in the same company as the well-known books that have received the Gallegos prize. In his forward to the 2012 edition, Larry McMurtry writes that the novel "is in its way a paean to the Venezuelan Llano" a vast grassy plain that, in the novel, "is steamy, tumescent, lust driven."<br /><br />In "Doña Barbara," the characters often say that the land does not relent. In fact, its horrible and fated barbarism and its magical and conscious beauty are as intertwined as surely as the destinies of the powerful female rancher Doña Barbara and the college educated and idealistic protagonist Santos Luzardos. When Luzardos returns to the Llano ranch, Altamira, of his distant youth, he finds that it has been depleted and nearly forsaken by incompetent, greedy overseers who couldn't resist their complicity in Doña's schemes. Through magic, sex, paid-off judges, and raw power, she has enlarged--at her neighbors' expense--her adjoining ranch El Miedo (fear) into a sprawling country unto itself that expands outward through manifest destiny.<br /><br />Readers may well be reminded of Luis Alberto Urrea's "The Hummingbird's Daughter" as they immerse their souls in "Doña Barbara," because both novels view the land in epic proportions, beautifully described and inseparable from the lives of its people. The land is essentially the main character or, at the very least, an infinite circle of hell where the well-drawn, multi-dimensional characters are driven to kill or be killed as fate decrees.<br /><br />Like two gladiators who have been chained together, Doña Barbara and Santos Luzardos cannot both survive in an enchanting, sadistic world where every action appears pre-ordained even when it isn't. The novel ends the only way it can, but the story's characters and readers won't realize this until they reach the last chapter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9050207666762092826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2036462219102642240"><blockquote>All things return whence they came.</blockquote><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598437455i/30017706._SX540_.jpg' width='623' height='471' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This book is such an unconventionally traditional book, with some rather modern adornations packed within savage, primitive setting. All of the characters represent through their behaviour, emotions and deeds some characteristic of this praerie they occupy: Doña Barbara represents pagan and barbarastic ways still practiced in the Amazonian area, but not without a note of emotions that just need to be stirred up and that stir up her environment; Santos Luzardo r</span>
  <span id="freeText2036462219102642240" style="display:none"><blockquote>All things return whence they came.</blockquote><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598437455i/30017706._SX540_.jpg" width="623" height="471" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This book is such an unconventionally traditional book, with some rather modern adornations packed within savage, primitive setting. All of the characters represent through their behaviour, emotions and deeds some characteristic of this praerie they occupy: Doña Barbara represents pagan and barbarastic ways still practiced in the Amazonian area, but not without a note of emotions that just need to be stirred up and that stir up her environment; Santos Luzardo represents progress and modern-thinking as way of saving the native folk (and himself) of primitive thinking; Altamira's peons represent loyalty, fiercesness and passion of native folk; Marisela represents gulliness and innocence but readiness to learn and better oneself and Señor Danger represents a foreigner who believes in white superiority and feels contempt for native folk. All of them get what they ultimately deserve and sometimes it was happy, sometimes heart-wrenching. <br>I came to understand and feel for Doña Barbara despite her savage ways and was a bit saddened that she did not come to experience true happiness. <br>But I guess since the characters are representing certain characteristics, it is only expected that barbarism and paganism were defeated. <br><br>I recommend reading this book as way of familiarizing with Venezuelan culture and history. It may be fiction but it is a good indication of how it was back then. <br><br>4,5/5 stars </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780226279206" href="/book/show/12839869-do-a-barbara">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2031662-peter?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2031662-peter?shelf=found-in-translation">found-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2031662-peter?shelf=round-the-world">round-the-world</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2874583161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13761171631870771443">Melodramatic and lots of fun; I can see why it made for a good tv series. And I can sort of see why it's regarded as a forerunner of South American magical realism, though actually its references to legends and the supernatural are quite interesting; Doña Barbara seems to deploy the idea of magic, and build herself as a legend, for strategic (though perhaps ultimately self-defeating) ends.<br/>My enjoyment was tempered by the fact that the novel struck me certainly early on (and still so, but less so</span>
  <span id="freeText13761171631870771443" style="display:none">Melodramatic and lots of fun; I can see why it made for a good tv series. And I can sort of see why it's regarded as a forerunner of South American magical realism, though actually its references to legends and the supernatural are quite interesting; Doña Barbara seems to deploy the idea of magic, and build herself as a legend, for strategic (though perhaps ultimately self-defeating) ends.<br />My enjoyment was tempered by the fact that the novel struck me certainly early on (and still so, but less so, as I read on) as terribly reactionary--certain descriptions seem to rely on racial theories, for one thing, and the whole plot is about whether or not the Plains will cause people--especially those with "Indian blood" in them--to be sucked into the quagmire of "barbarism," even when they have the best, most restrained, law-abiding, bourgeois intentions. But the division between civilized and barbaric gets complicated over the course of the novel. Running alongside and through this fear (but also admiration) of barbarism runs the idea that women need to be domesticated and tamed, steered toward tenderness and not "unnatural" unruliness. The very fact there is a Doña in charge is a sign that the universe is out of kilter at the start of the book. But that taming narrative only plays out so far, I think. There's still plenty of cover to enjoy the lawlessness of Doña Barbara and her daughter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13761171631870771443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer428610427641158416">This is the Venezuelan novel that introduced magical realism to literature. The author went on to become President of Venezuela. Since Venezuela is one more of those tropical nations colonized by the Spanish, it shares many political and economic characteristics with the Philippines. The political corruption, land distribution methods, Inquisition era Catholicism combined with traditional beliefs, and subsistence farming read very much like life in the Philippines. The story was interesting and </span>
  <span id="freeText428610427641158416" style="display:none">This is the Venezuelan novel that introduced magical realism to literature. The author went on to become President of Venezuela. Since Venezuela is one more of those tropical nations colonized by the Spanish, it shares many political and economic characteristics with the Philippines. The political corruption, land distribution methods, Inquisition era Catholicism combined with traditional beliefs, and subsistence farming read very much like life in the Philippines. The story was interesting and familiar. The only book left for me to read from The Great American Read list (Think that's the name.) I've already started White Teeth. The ones on the list that will remain unread are ones that don't interest me at all... 50 Shades, Left Behind, and CSLewis stuff.<br />BTW, slowly reading Finnegans Wake. No hurry with that, plus, I'm reading the Skeleton Key as I go along... matching one chapter to one chapter as I go. I did find an audio recording of Joyce reading the Anna Livia Pulurablla piece aloud. Wow! This was helpful in reading the book... if in doubt, just get the iambic pentameter going and read it aloud as a chant. </span>

  <span id="freeText6588898065254883425" style="display:none"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[People believe Doña Barbara is a witch, a sorceress. She tends to believe it herself, as the universe reorders her world to suit her wishes. The neighbors' land, their livestock, their men--all become hers. <br /><br />The surviving heir of one of the dispossessed neighbors, Santos Luzardo, comes home from Caracas, determined to repossess his birthright; and Doña Barbara determines to possess him. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Read for a couple of 2019 reading challenges, this book was on the 2018 Great American Reads list. There is a relatively famous Spanish-language theatrical release from 1943 (the book was published in 1929): <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0035825/?ref_=nm_flmg_wr_11">https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0035825/...</a><br />(Took an extra week to read this: I had my appendix out and wasn't reading between January 18 and 22.)<br /><br />I have failed to fall for the charms of other magical realism books, but I enjoyed this very much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6588898065254883425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12523293868850125657">Such a pleasant surprise I found in reading this book! It’s the 100th on the Great American Read List, and the blurb about it didn’t sound exciting to me at all, but I was captivated from the very first page. The writing (translation) is beautiful, with heartfelt prose that transported me into the plains for Venezuela.   <span id="freeText12523293868850125657" style="display:none">Such a pleasant surprise I found in reading this book! It’s the 100th on the Great American Read List, and the blurb about it didn’t sound exciting to me at all, but I was captivated from the very first page. The writing (translation) is beautiful, with heartfelt prose that transported me into the plains for Venezuela. Plainsmen, cowboys, cattle and horses and the age-old fights over land boundaries, mixed in with conflict between barbarism and civilization, make for an engaging and eventful read. And ultimately, the complex character of Dona Barbara, created from her hate for men after losing her lover, and ultimately changed by an unattainable love, make this a book well worth reading and discussing. Highly recommend. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10600484030031849298">I chose this book because it was on the Great American Read list. Doña Bárbara is a novel by Venezuelan author Rómulo Gallegos, first published in 1929. This regionalist novel deals with the confrontation between civilization and the barbaric aspects of the rural environment and its inhabitants. The style of writing was unusual and dithered along with lengthy descriptions of either the landscape or the character's thoughts. The heroine, Dona Barbara, is a powerful despicable woman, and the hero </span>
  <span id="freeText10600484030031849298" style="display:none">I chose this book because it was on the Great American Read list. Doña Bárbara is a novel by Venezuelan author Rómulo Gallegos, first published in 1929. This regionalist novel deals with the confrontation between civilization and the barbaric aspects of the rural environment and its inhabitants. The style of writing was unusual and dithered along with lengthy descriptions of either the landscape or the character's thoughts. The heroine, Dona Barbara, is a powerful despicable woman, and the hero is a goody-two shoes who returns to his childhood home and fights the corruption he finds there. I found it to be laborious and found myself skipping paragraphs thinking, get on with it already!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4337493122589513750">Doña Barbara by Rómulo Gallegos <br/>Number 100 on The Great American Read. This story has been called an old gothic. If you want a book the cruises the vistas for long stretches then this maybe something for you to read.   <span id="freeText4337493122589513750" style="display:none">Doña Barbara by Rómulo Gallegos <br />Number 100 on The Great American Read. This story has been called an old gothic. I definitely had the same feeling I had reading this book as I’ve had on my several attempts at reading Dracula. If you want a book the cruises the vistas for long stretches then this maybe something for you to read. I’m not well versed in gothic literature but it seems that this old style of writing really hooks you in with a build up. I had a sense from the start of the book that things were going to come to a head and while they do, it may not be in the way you might have expected.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+mq6TwJLkJU3ZUJxvLJhhzz7O9ZfBtaA9RIADLKnoS2x4fJIsXj7onkUJ//ECXitA2xpQ+b9mxCTMw+NaPkdww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }
